Let me think about what's actually involved.\n  1315\t\n  1316\tApproach 3 requires:\n  1317\t\n  1318\t1.  The model can pause mid-generation\n  1319\t2.  Signal that it needs more context\n  1320\t3.  Receive new context\n  1321\t4.  Resume generation incorporating that context\n  1322\t\n  1323\tLet me think about what this would require at the model/inference level and whether open weight models make this feasible.\n  1324\t\n  1325\tKey considerations:\n  1326\t\n  1327\t*   Transformer architecture and KV cache\n  1328\t*   The ability to inject tokens mid-sequence\n  1329\t*   Whether this requires architectural changes vs inference engine changes\n  1330\t*   Open weight models like Llama, Mistral, Qwen etc.\n  1331\t*   Inference frameworks like vLLM, llama.cpp, SGLang, etc.\n  1332\t\n  1333\tThe fundamental question is: can you inject new context tokens into the middle of generation and continue? The KV cache is the key constraint here. If I want to inject new context mid-generation, I could stop generation, prepend or append new context to the existing sequence, and then recompute \u2014 but that's computationally expensive and might not preserve the generation quality.\n  1334\t\n  1335\tWith open weight models and direct control over the inference loop, I have more flexibility. I can pause generation at any point, detect special tokens or patterns, modify the context or KV cache, and resume. The key insight is that I own the entire inference pipeline, so I can monitor token generation in real-time and intervene as needed. This makes options like pausing and retrieving new context, then continuing with KV cache reuse for the unchanged prefix, very feasible.\n  1336\t\n  1337\tThe concrete approach would be to fine-tune the model to emit a special \"RETRIEVE\" token when it needs external information, then in the inference loop, detect that token, pause generation, run the retrieval query, inject the results into the context, and continue. The main technical challenge is handling the KV cache when injecting new context\u2014the attention patterns for subsequent tokens need to attend to the newly added context. The simplest solution is to treat everything before the injection point as a reusable prefix, append the new context, compute KV entries for just those new tokens, and continue autoregressive generation. This aligns naturally with how transformers work. For fine-tuning, I'd need training data that demonstrates the suspend-and-retrieve pattern so the model learns when to emit the special token.\n  1338\t\n  1339\tI'm also considering existing work in this space\u2014SGLang has interesting primitives for controlling generation flow, and there's active retrieval augmented generation research like FLARE that tackles similar problems at a higher level. This approach is actually quite feasible with LoRA or full fine-tuning on open-weight models, though I should be honest about the technical requirements and potential risks involved.\n  1340\t\n  1341\tShow more\n  1342\t\n  1343\tDone\n  1344\t\n  1345\tYes, and this is one of the few cases where open weights give you a capability that's genuinely unavailable through commercial APIs. Let me be precise about what's required and where the hard problems are.\n  1346\t\n  1347\tWhy Open Weights Make This Possible\n  1348\t-----------------------------------\n  1349\t\n  1350\tThe co-routine architecture requires breaking the atomicity of inference. With a commercial API, inference is a black box \u2014 tokens go in, tokens come out. You cannot intervene mid-generation. With open weights and control of the inference loop, you can. You own the forward pass. You decide what happens between every token.\n  1351\t\n  1352\tThe fundamental mechanism is straightforward in principle:\n  1353\t\n  1354\t    def coroutine_inference(model, kv_cache, context_tokens):\n  1355\t        while not done:\n  1356\t            logits = model.forward(current_token, kv_cache)\n  1357\t            next_token = sample(logits)\n  1358\t            \n  1359\t            if next_token == RETRIEVE_SIGNAL:\n  1360\t                # PAUSE: hand control to System 1\n  1361\t                cue = extract_cue(generated_so_far)\n  1362\t                new_context = S1(fact_store, cue)\n  1363\t                \n  1364\t                # INJECT: extend context, update KV cache\n  1365\t                new_tokens = tokenize(new_context)\n  1366\t                for t in new_tokens:\n  1367\t                    model.forward(t, kv_cache)  # build KV entries\n  1368\t                \n  1369\t                # RESUME: continue generation with enriched context\n  1370\t                continue\n  1371\t            \n  1372\t            yield next_token\n  1373\t\n  1374\tThis is not exotic. It's a modification of the standard autoregressive generation loop that every inference framework already implements. The architectural novelty is in two places: teaching the model to emit the retrieval signal, and handling the KV cache correctly when context is injected.\n  1375\t\n  1376\tThe Three Technical Challenges\n  1377\t------------------------------\n  1378\t\n  1379\t### Challenge 1: The Retrieval Signal\n  1380\t\n  1381\tThe model needs to learn to emit a special token (or token sequence) that means \"I need more context before continuing.\" This is the learned analogue of Kahneman's System 2 recognizing its own confusion.\n  1382\t\n  1383\tThere are several approaches, in order of increasing investment:\n  1384\t\n  1385\t**Approach A \u2014 Reserve a special token.** Add a `<RETRIEVE>` token to the vocabulary (or repurpose an unused special token). Fine-tune the model on data where this token appears at points where retrieval would have helped. The training data looks like:\n  1386\t\n  1387\t    [context] The function handle_request calls validate_input, \n  1388\t    which according to <RETRIEVE>\n  1389\t    [injected: the validate_input implementation from the codebase]\n  1390\t    the schema defined in models.py, expects a JSON payload with...\n  1391\t\n  1392\tThe model learns that `<RETRIEVE>` is a natural point to pause, and that useful context will appear after it. This is essentially how tool-calling was trained into models \u2014 the same mechanism, different semantics.\n  1393\t\n  1394\t**Approach B \u2014 Use logprob entropy as an implicit signal.** Don't add a token. Instead, monitor the entropy of the output distribution at each step in the generation loop:\n  1395\t\n  1396\t    logits = model.forward(current_token, kv_cache)\n  1397\t    entropy = -sum(softmax(logits) * log_softmax(logits))\n  1398\t    \n  1399\t    if entropy > threshold:\n  1400\t        # Model is uncertain \u2014 trigger retrieval\n  1401\t        cue = extract_cue(generated_so_far)\n  1402\t        ...\n  1403\t\n  1404\tThis requires no fine-tuning. The insight is that high entropy _is_ the confusion signal \u2014 the model doesn't know what token to produce, which means it lacks the information to make a confident decision. The threshold needs calibration, and not all high-entropy points are retrieval-worthy (some are just stylistic optionality), but as a zero-training starting point this is testable immediately.\n  1405\t\n  1406\t**Approach C \u2014 Fine-tune a separate classifier head.** Attach a small binary classification head to the model's hidden states that predicts \"should I retrieve right now?\" at each token position. This is trained as a lightweight auxiliary objective and doesn't modify the main model weights at all:\n  1407\t\n  1408\t    hidden_state = model.get_hidden_state(current_token, kv_cache)\n  1409\t    should_retrieve = classifier_head(hidden_state)  # small MLP\n  1410\t\n  1411\tThis is more principled than entropy thresholding because it can learn _which kinds_ of uncertainty warrant retrieval versus which are benign.\n  1412\t\n  1413\t### Challenge 2: KV Cache Management\n  1414\t\n  1415\tThis is the part that requires careful engineering. The KV cache is the transformer's working memory \u2014 it stores the key-value attention pairs for all tokens processed so far. When you inject new context mid-generation, you need to integrate it correctly.\n  1416\t\n  1417\tThe good news: the standard transformer architecture handles this naturally. The KV cache is a sequence. You can extend it by running forward passes over the new context tokens. The subsequent generated tokens will attend to both the original context and the injected material.\n  1418\t\n  1419\t    # State before injection:\n  1420\t    KV = [ctx_1, ctx_2, ..., ctx_n, gen_1, gen_2, ..., gen_k]\n  1421\t    \n  1422\t    # After injecting new context:\n  1423\t    for t in new_context_tokens:\n  1424\t        model.forward(t, KV)  # appends to KV\n  1425\t    \n  1426\t    # KV is now:\n  1427\t    KV = [ctx_1, ..., ctx_n, gen_1, ..., gen_k, new_1, ..., new_m]\n  1428\t    \n  1429\t    # Generation continues, attending to everything:\n  1430\t    next_token = sample(model.forward(gen_k, KV))\n  1431\t\n\t< truncated lines 1432-1568 >\n  1569\t    Labels = Attributes\n  1570\t\n  1571\tAn entity like `file:main.py` has edges labeled `last-modified`, `contains-function`, `last-error`, etc., pointing to values. Some of those values are themselves entities \u2014 `contains-function` might point to `function:handle_request`, which has its own attribute edges (`called-by`, `raises-error`, `defined-at-line`, etc.).\n  1572\t\n  1573\tThe `associate` tool performs a **bounded traversal** of this graph from seed nodes determined by semantic context:\n  1574\t\n  1575\t    associate(context, depth, breadth):\n  1576\t      seeds = semantic_match(context, all_entities)  -- embedding similarity\n  1577\t      \n  1578\t      neighborhood = {}\n  1579\t      frontier = seeds\n  1580\t      for d in 1..depth:\n  1581\t        for entity in frontier:\n  1582\t          attrs = get_attributes(entity)  -- all attribute labels on this entity\n  1583\t          neighborhood[entity] = attrs\n  1584\t          \n  1585\t          -- Follow edges to related entities (breadth-limited)\n  1586\t          related = [v for (e, a, v) in facts \n  1587\t                     if e == entity and is_entity(v)]\n  1588\t          frontier = top_k(related, breadth)  -- limit fan-out\n  1589\t      \n  1590\t      return neighborhood\n  1591\t\n  1592\tThe return value is not data \u2014 it's **shape**. The LLM receives something like:\n  1593\t\n  1594\t    Relevant schema neighborhood (depth=2):\n  1595\t    \n  1596\t    file:main.py\n  1597\t      \u251c\u2500 last-modified (timestamp)\n  1598\t      \u251c\u2500 contains-function (\u2192 entity)\n  1599\t      \u251c\u2500 last-error (text)\n  1600\t      \u2514\u2500 line-count (integer)\n  1601\t    \n  1602\t    function:handle_request  [via file:main.py \u2192 contains-function]\n  1603\t      \u251c\u2500 called-by (\u2192 entity)\n  1604\t      \u251c\u2500 raises-error (\u2192 entity)\n  1605\t      \u251c\u2500 param-types (text)\n  1606\t      \u2514\u2500 last-tested (timestamp)\n  1607\t    \n  1608\t    error:type-error-42  [via function:handle_request \u2192 raises-error]\n  1609\t      \u251c\u2500 message (text)\n  1610\t      \u251c\u2500 stack-trace (text)\n  1611\t      \u2514\u2500 first-seen (timestamp)\n  1612\t\n  1613\tThis is a **map of what's knowable**, not the knowledge itself. The token cost is low \u2014 you're transmitting attribute names and types, not values. Thirty attributes with entity paths might be 200-300 tokens. And it gives the LLM exactly what it needs to formulate a precise query.\n  1614\t\n  1615\tWhy This Is Deeply Right\n  1616\t------------------------\n  1617\t\n  1618\tThree reasons, in increasing depth:\n  1619\t\n  1620\t**Reason 1 \u2014 It solves the query generation problem cheaply.** We identified earlier that the hard part of the EAV architecture is `query_for` \u2014 who writes the queries? With `associate`, the LLM writes the queries, but it's not doing it blind. It's inspecting a menu. This converts query generation from an open-ended generation problem (hard, error-prone) to a **selection and composition problem** (much easier for LLMs). The LLM is good at \"given these available attributes, which combination answers my question?\" It's bad at \"guess what attributes might exist in a store you've never seen.\"\n  1621\t\n  1622\t**Reason 2 \u2014 It's the System 1 analogue of peripheral vision.** In Kahneman's framework, System 1 doesn't just retrieve specific memories \u2014 it maintains a **peripheral awareness** of what's accessible. You're not thinking about your car keys, but if someone mentions \"keys\" you immediately know you have knowledge about where they are. `associate` gives the LLM peripheral awareness of the fact store's schema neighborhood. It doesn't load the data (that would be expensive), but it makes the LLM aware that the data _exists and is loadable_. This is precisely the priming mechanism that makes human associative memory so effective.\n  1623\t\n  1624\t**Reason 3 \u2014 It's a natural transformation between two categories.** This is the algebraic point. We have two categories in play:\n  1625\t\n  1626\t    Category 1 (Semantic): Objects are semantic contexts, \n  1627\t      morphisms are similarity/association relations. \n  1628\t      Unstructured, continuous, embedding-space.\n  1629\t    \n  1630\t    Category 2 (Structural): Objects are EAV schema neighborhoods, \n  1631\t      morphisms are Datalog queries. \n  1632\t      Structured, discrete, logic-space.\n  1633\t\n  1634\tThe `associate` tool is a **functor** from Category 1 to Category 2. It translates semantic proximity (\"I'm working on error handling in main.py\") into structural adjacency (the attribute graph neighborhood of the relevant entities). The LLM then operates entirely within Category 2 to construct queries.\n  1635\t\n  1636\tWithout `associate`, the LLM has to perform this cross-category translation itself, implicitly, with no grounding in what actually exists in the store. With `associate`, the translation is performed by a dedicated mechanism, and the LLM receives the result as structured input. This is a clean separation of concerns.\n  1637\t\n  1638\tThe Depth/Breadth Parameters\n  1639\t----------------------------\n  1640\t\n  1641\tYour intuition to parameterize the traversal is important. Different situations call for different exploration strategies:\n  1642\t\n  1643\t    associate(context, depth=1, breadth=\u221e)\n  1644\t      -- \"Show me everything about the entities I'm directly working with\"\n  1645\t      -- Useful for: tactical, focused work on known entities\n  1646\t    \n  1647\t    associate(context, depth=3, breadth=3)  \n  1648\t      -- \"Show me the broader neighborhood, but don't explode\"\n  1649\t      -- Useful for: discovering unexpected connections,\n  1650\t      --   e.g. \"this error is related to a config change \n  1651\t      --   two degrees of association away\"\n  1652\t    \n  1653\t    associate(context, depth=1, breadth=\u221e) \n  1654\t      then on confusion:\n  1655\t    associate(confusion_context, depth=3, breadth=5)\n  1656\t      -- Start narrow, widen on confusion\n  1657\t      -- This IS the System 2 \u2192 System 1 feedback loop\n  1658\t\n  1659\tAnd here's the connection back to Kahneman that I think clinches it: **the depth/breadth parameters are the attentional aperture**. Focused attention is depth=1, broad awareness is depth=3+. The transition from focused to broad is exactly what happens when System 2 encounters surprise \u2014 attention widens, more associations become accessible, and previously irrelevant connections surface.\n  1660\t\n  1661\tThe meta-policy `\u03bc` we defined earlier now has a concrete lever:\n  1662\t\n  1663\t    \u03bc : Task \u00d7 ConfusionLevel \u2192 (depth, breadth)\n  1664\t\n  1665\tLow confusion \u2192 narrow, deep. High confusion \u2192 broad, shallow. This is a one-dimensional control surface that modulates the entire retrieval architecture.\n  1666\t\n  1667\tOne Design Question\n  1668\t-------------------\n  1669\t\n  1670\tShould `associate` do the semantic matching internally (using embeddings to find seed entities), or should the LLM provide explicit seed entities? Two options:\n  1671\t\n  1672\t**Option A \u2014 Semantic seeds (automatic):**\n  1673\t\n  1674\t    associate(\"I'm debugging the TypeError in handle_request\")\n  1675\t    \u2192 internally embeds this, finds nearest entities, traverses\n  1676\t\n  1677\t**Option B \u2014 Explicit seeds (LLM-directed):**\n  1678\t\n  1679\t    associate(entities=[\"function:handle_request\", \"error:type-error-42\"], \n  1680\t              depth=2, breadth=5)\n  1681\t    \u2192 traverses from specified roots\n  1682\t\n  1683\tOption A is more System-1-like \u2014 the association is automatic, subconscious. Option B gives the LLM more control but requires it to already know entity identifiers. My instinct is that you want both \u2014 Option A as the default (fired automatically on each cycle, low cost, peripheral awareness), and Option B as a targeted tool the LLM can invoke explicitly when it knows what it's looking for.\n  1684\t\n  1685\tWhat's your read?\n  1686\t\n  1687\t---\n  1688\t\n  1689\t## You asked:\n  1690\t\n  1691\tI agree. The training data looks like:\\n\\n    [context] The function handle_request calls validate_input, \\n    which according to <RETRIEVE>\\n    [injected: the validate_input implementation from the codebase]\\n    the schema defined in models.py, expects a JSON payload with...\\n\\nThe model learns that `<RETRIEVE>` is a natural point to pause, and that useful context will appear after it. This is essentially how tool-calling was trained into models \\u2014 the same mechanism, different semantics.\\n\\n**Approach B \\u2014 Use logprob entropy as an implicit signal.** Don't add a token. Instead, monitor the entropy of the output distribution at each step in the generation loop:\\n\\n    logits = model.forward(current_token, kv_cache)\\n    entropy = -sum(softmax(logits) * log_softmax(logits))\\n    \\n    if entropy > threshold:\\n        # Model is uncertain \\u2014 trigger retrieval\\n        cue = extract_cue(generated_so_far)\\n        ...\\n\\nThis requires no fine-tuning. The insight is that high entropy _is_ the confusion signal \\u2014 the model doesn't know what token to produce, which means it lacks the information to make a confident decision. The threshold needs calibration, and not all high-entropy points are retrieval-worthy (some are just stylistic optionality), but as a zero-training starting point this is testable immediately.\\n\\n**Approach C \\u2014 Fine-tune a separate classifier head.** Attach a small binary classification head to the model's hidden states that predicts \\\"should I retrieve right now?\\\" at each token position. This is trained as a lightweight auxiliary objective and doesn't modify the main model weights at all:\\n\\n    hidden_state = model.get_hidden_state(current_token, kv_cache)\\n    should_retrieve = classifier_head(hidden_state)  # small MLP\\n\\nThis is more principled than entropy thresholding because it can learn _which kinds_ of uncertainty warrant retrieval versus which are benign.\\n\\n### Challenge 2: KV Cache Management\\n\\nThis is the part that requires careful engineering. The KV cache is the transformer's working memory \\u2014 it stores the key-value attention pairs for all tokens processed so far. When you inject new context mid-generation, you need to integrate it correctly.\\n\\nThe good news: the standard transformer architecture handles this naturally. The KV cache is a sequence. You can extend it by running forward passes over the new context tokens. The subsequent generated tokens will attend to both the original context and the injected material.\\n\\n    # State before injection:\\n    KV = [ctx_1, ctx_2, ..., ctx_n, gen_1, gen_2, ..., gen_k]\\n    \\n    # After injecting new context:\\n    for t in new_context_tokens:\\n        model.forward(t, KV)  # appends to KV\\n    \\n    # KV is now:\\n    KV = [ctx_1, ..., ctx_n, gen_1, ..., gen_k, new_1, ..., new_m]\\n    \\n    # Generation continues, attending to everything:\\n    next_token = sample(model.forward(gen_k, KV))\\n\\n< truncated lines 1432-1568 >\\n    Labels = Attributes\\n\\nAn entity like `file:main.py` has edges labeled `last-modified`, `contains-function`, `last-error`, etc., pointing to values. Some of those values are themselves entities \\u2014 `contains-function` might point to `function:handle_request`, which has its own attribute edges (`called-by`, `raises-error`, `defined-at-line`, etc.).\\n\\nThe `associate` tool performs a **bounded traversal** of this graph from seed nodes determined by semantic context:\\n\\n    associate(context, depth, breadth):\\n      seeds = semantic_match(context, all_entities)  -- embedding similarity\\n      \\n      neighborhood = {}\\n      frontier = seeds\\n      for d in 1..depth:\\n        for entity in frontier:\\n          attrs = get_attributes(entity)  -- all attribute labels on this entity\\n          neighborhood[entity] = attrs\\n          \\n          -- Follow edges to related entities (breadth-limited)\\n          related = [v for (e, a, v) in facts \\n                     if e == entity and is_entity(v)]\\n          frontier = top_k(related, breadth)  -- limit fan-out\\n      \\n      return neighborhood\\n\\nThe return value is not data \\u2014 it's **shape**. The LLM receives something like:\\n\\n    Relevant schema neighborhood (depth=2):\\n    \\n    file:main.py\\n      \\u251c\\u2500 last-modified (timestamp)\\n      \\u251c\\u2500 contains-function (\\u2192 entity)\\n      \\u251c\\u2500 last-error (text)\\n      \\u2514\\u2500 line-count (integer)\\n    \\n    function:handle_request  [via file:main.py \\u2192 contains-function]\\n      \\u251c\\u2500 called-by (\\u2192 entity)\\n      \\u251c\\u2500 raises-error (\\u2192 entity)\\n      \\u251c\\u2500 param-types (text)\\n      \\u2514\\u2500 last-tested (timestamp)\\n    \\n    error:type-error-42  [via function:handle_request \\u2192 raises-error]\\n      \\u251c\\u2500 message (text)\\n      \\u251c\\u2500 stack-trace (text)\\n      \\u2514\\u2500 first-seen (timestamp)\\n\\nThis is a **map of what's knowable**, not the knowledge itself. The token cost is low \\u2014 you're transmitting attribute names and types, not values. Thirty attributes with entity paths might be 200-300 tokens. And it gives the LLM exactly what it needs to formulate a precise query.\\n\\nWhy This Is Deeply Right\\n------------------------\\n\\nThree reasons, in increasing depth:\\n\\n**Reason 1 \\u2014 It solves the query generation problem cheaply.** We identified earlier that the hard part of the EAV architecture is `query_for` \\u2014 who writes the queries? With `associate`, the LLM writes the queries, but it's not doing it blind. It's inspecting a menu. This converts query generation from an open-ended generation problem (hard, error-prone) to a **selection and composition problem** (much easier for LLMs). The LLM is good at \\\"given these available attributes, which combination answers my question?\\\" It's bad at \\\"guess what attributes might exist in a store you've never seen.\\\"\\n\\n**Reason 2 \\u2014 It's the System 1 analogue of peripheral vision.** In Kahneman's framework, System 1 doesn't just retrieve specific memories \\u2014 it maintains a **peripheral awareness** of what's accessible. You're not thinking about your car keys, but if someone mentions \\\"keys\\\" you immediately know you have knowledge about where they are. `associate` gives the LLM peripheral awareness of the fact store's schema neighborhood. It doesn't load the data (that would be expensive), but it makes the LLM aware that the data _exists and is loadable_. This is precisely the priming mechanism that makes human associative memory so effective.\\n\\n**Reason 3 \\u2014 It's a natural transformation between two categories.** This is the algebraic point. We have two categories in play:\\n\\n    Category 1 (Semantic): Objects are semantic contexts, \\n      morphisms are similarity/association relations. \\n      Unstructured, continuous, embedding-space.\\n    \\n    Category 2 (Structural): Objects are EAV schema neighborhoods, \\n      morphisms are Datalog queries. \\n      Structured, discrete, logic-space.\\n\\nThe `associate` tool is a **functor** from Category 1 to Category 2. It translates semantic proximity (\\\"I'm working on error handling in main.py\\\") into structural adjacency (the attribute graph neighborhood of the relevant entities). The LLM then operates entirely within Category 2 to construct queries.\\n\\nWithout `associate`, the LLM has to perform this cross-category translation itself, implicitly, with no grounding in what actually exists in the store. With `associate`, the translation is performed by a dedicated mechanism, and the LLM receives the result as structured input. This is a clean separation of concerns.\\n\\nThe Depth/Breadth Parameters\\n----------------------------\\n\\nYour intuition to parameterize the traversal is important. Different situations call for different exploration strategies:\\n\\n    associate(context, depth=1, breadth=\\u221e)\\n      -- \\\"Show me everything about the entities I'm directly working with\\\"\\n      -- Useful for: tactical, focused work on known entities\\n    \\n    associate(context, depth=3, breadth=3)  \\n      -- \\\"Show me the broader neighborhood, but don't explode\\\"\\n      -- Useful for: discovering unexpected connections,\\n      --   e.g. \\\"this error is related to a config change \\n      --   two degrees of association away\\\"\\n    \\n    associate(context, depth=1, breadth=\\u221e) \\n      then on confusion:\\n    associate(confusion_context, depth=3, breadth=5)\\n      -- Start narrow, widen on confusion\\n      -- This IS the System 2 \\u2192 System 1 feedback loop\\n\\nAnd here's the connection back to Kahneman that I think clinches it: **the depth/breadth parameters are the attentional aperture**. Focused attention is depth=1, broad awareness is depth=3+. The transition from focused to broad is exactly what happens when System 2 encounters surprise \\u2014 attention widens, more associations become accessible, and previously irrelevant connections surface.\\n\\nThe meta-policy `\\u03bc` we defined earlier now has a concrete lever:\\n\\n    \\u03bc : Task \\u00d7 ConfusionLevel \\u2192 (depth, breadth)\\n\\nLow confusion \\u2192 narrow, deep. High confusion \\u2192 broad, shallow. This is a one-dimensional control surface that modulates the entire retrieval architecture.\\n\\nOne Design Question\\n-------------------\\n\\nShould `associate` do the semantic matching internally (using embeddings to find seed entities), or should the LLM provide explicit seed entities? Two options:\\n\\n**Option A \\u2014 Semantic seeds (automatic):**\\n\\n    associate(\\\"I'm debugging the TypeError in handle_request\\\")\\n    \\u2192 internally embeds this, finds nearest entities, traverses\\n\\n**Option B \\u2014 Explicit seeds (LLM-directed):**\\n\\n    associate(entities=[\\\"function:handle_request\\\", \\\"error:type-error-42\\\"], \\n              depth=2, breadth=5)\\n    \\u2192 traverses from specified roots\\n\\nOption A is more System-1-like \\u2014 the association is automatic, subconscious. Option B gives the LLM more control but requires it to already know entity identifiers. My instinct is that you want both \\u2014 Option A as the default (fired automatically on each cycle, low cost, peripheral awareness), and Option B as a targeted tool the LLM can invoke explicitly when it knows what it's looking for.\\n\\nWhat's your read?\\n\\n---\\n\\n## You asked:\\n\\nI agree. Models with **rotary positional embeddings** (RoPE) \\u2014 which includes LLaMA, Mistral, Qwen, and most modern open architectures \\u2014 are more forgiving here because RoPE encodes relative position, and attention can still learn to treat the injected material as reference context.\\n\\nHowever, for optimal behavior you might want a more sophisticated injection strategy:\\n\\n**Option 1 \\u2014 Append and continue** (simplest). The cost is proportional to the number of tokens generated before injection \\u2014 for short generation bursts (which is what you'd expect if the model retrieves early when confused), this is modest.\\n\\n**Option 3 \\u2014 Prefix insertion with KV surgery.** Directly splice new KV entries into the middle of the cache at the appropriate position. This is technically possible but fragile and framework-dependent. I'd avoid it unless performance demands it.\\n\\n### Challenge 3: Training Data\\n\\nThis is the least technical but possibly the hardest problem in practice. You need training data that exhibits the pattern: \\\"model generates, pauses, receives context, continues better than it would have without the context.\\\"\\n\\nOne way to generate this data synthetically:\\n\\n1. Construct counterfactual traces where the model pauses at the right point, receives the missing context, and produces the correct continuation.\\n4. The later stages improve precision and expressiveness, but the core mechanism \\u2014 detect confusion, retrieve, inject, continue \\u2014 is present from Stage 0.\\n\\nThe Honest Uncertainty\\n----------------------\\n\\nTwo things I'm genuinely unsure about:\\n\\n**Will the model handle mid-stream context injection gracefully?** The model was trained on contiguous text. Injecting context mid-generation creates a sequence structure it has never seen during training. With fine-tuning (Stage 2) this should resolve, but at Stage 1 with just entropy detection and raw injection, the model might produce incoherent output after the injection point. The severity of this depends on the model and the injection strategy. I'd want to test it empirically before committing.\\n\\n**Is per-token entropy actually a good confusion signal?** It's theoretically motivated but I don't have empirical data. High entropy might correlate more with \\\"many valid continuations\\\" (stylistic optionality) than with \\\"missing information\\\" (genuine confusion). The signal-to-noise ratio is an empirical question. If it's too noisy, you'd need to jump to the classifier head approach (Challenge 1, Approach C) earlier than I've staged it.\\n\\nBoth of these are answerable with experiments, not more theorizing. Would you want to sketch out what those experiments would look like, or do you want to continue deepening the formal architecture first?\\n\\n---\\n\\n## You asked:\\n\\nOk I have another idea: a tool like \\u201cassociate\\u201d that gives relevant eav attributes based on semantic context. This might be 20 or 30 attributes (possibly with a breadth/depth value for how many transitive associations away from the root semantic eav nodes). This might prime the otherwise unaware ai of data shapes available to it that it might be able to query for in datalog (using another tool most likely)\\n\\n1:07 AM\\n\\n---\\n\\n## Claude Replied:\\n\\nValidated two-phase retrieval strategy for schema discovery and querying\\n\\nValidated two-phase retrieval strategy for schema discovery and querying\\n\\nThe user is proposing a tool called \\\"associate\\\" that acts as a System 1 primitive \\u2014 given the current semantic context, it returns relevant EAV attributes (not values, just the schema/shape) from the fact store. This primes the LLM to know what's queryable, solving the cold-start problem of \\\"how does the LLM know what to ask for if it doesn't know what's in the store?\\\"\\n\\nThis is elegant. Let me think through why.\\n\\nThe problem it solves: the LLM can't write Datalog queries against a schema it doesn't know exists. The associate tool surfaces the schema neighborhood relevant to the current context, and then the LLM can write precise queries using a second tool.\\n\\nSo it's a two-phase retrieval pattern \\u2014 first discover what's available through associate, then fetch what's needed with a query tool. The breadth and depth parameters control how far to traverse the entity-attribute graph, essentially tuning how much of the schema neighborhood gets exposed.\\n\\nShow more\\n\\nDone\\n\\nThis is a genuinely elegant idea and I want to be precise about _why_ it's elegant, because I think it solves a problem we hadn't explicitly named yet.\\n\\nThe Cold Start Problem\\n----------------------\\n\\nThere's a bootstrapping issue we glossed over in the dual-process architecture. When System 2 (the LLM) needs to generate a Datalog query against the fact store, it faces a prerequisite question: **what is there to query?**\\n\\nIn a fixed-schema system this is trivial \\u2014 the schema is known at design time and can be baked into the system prompt. But we specifically chose EAV _because_ the schema evolves at runtime. The set of entities and attributes grows as the agent works. The LLM can't write a query for `(:attribute :last-error)` if it doesn't know that attribute exists in the store.\\n\\nThis is the epistemological equivalent of: you can't ask a question about something you don't know exists. And this is exactly what `associate` solves. It's not retrieval \\u2014 it's **pre-retrieval**. It answers the question \\\"what questions are available to ask?\\\" before the LLM commits to asking one.\\n\\nThe Two-Phase Retrieval Pattern\\n-------------------------------\\n\\nWhat you're describing is a factoring of System 1 into two distinct operations:\\n\\n    Phase 1 \\u2014 associate : SemanticContext \\u2192 [Attribute]\\n      \\\"Given what I'm currently thinking about, what kinds of \\n       facts exist in the store that might be relevant?\\\"\\n       \\n    Phase 2 \\u2014 query : Datalog \\u2192 [Datom]  \\n      \\\"Now that I know what's available, give me the specific \\n       facts I need.\\\"\\n\\nThis is a **funnel**: broad semantic association narrowing to precise structured retrieval. The LLM's role sits _between_ the two phases \\u2014 it receives the attribute landscape from `associate`, reasons about which attributes are relevant to its current task, and constructs a precise `query` call.\\n\\n    S1_phase1: associate(semantic_context, depth=2) \\n               \\u2192 schema neighborhood\\n    \\n         \\u2193 LLM inspects schema neighborhood, decides what to query\\n    \\n    S1_phase2: query([:find ?e ?v :where [?e :attr ?v] ...]) \\n               \\u2192 concrete facts\\n    \\n         \\u2193 Facts assembled into \\u03a8\\n    \\n    S2: \\u03c0(\\u03a8) \\u2192 action\\n\\nThe depth/breadth parameter you've described controls the traversal of the **entity-attribute graph** in the fact store. Let me formalize this.\\n\\nThe EAV Store as a Graph\\n------------------------\\n\\nAn EAV fact store is naturally a **labeled directed graph**:\\n\\n    Nodes  = Entities \\u222a Values\\n    Edges  = {(e, a, v) | (e, a, v, t, true) \\u2208 FactStore}\\n\", \"filename\": \"/mnt/user-data/uploads/Formal_analysis_of_agentic_system_architecture.md\"}"

Let me think deeply about what's missing and what would be most transformative.\n  1704\t\n  1705\tWe have:\n  1706\t\n  1707\t*   EAV fact store\n  1708\t*   Dual-process S1/S2 architecture\n  1709\t*   Associate tool for schema discovery\n  1710\t*   Query tool for precise retrieval\n  1711\t*   Confusion feedback loop\n  1712\t*   Depth/breadth attentional aperture\n  1713\t\n  1714\tWhat's the biggest gap? Let me think...\n  1715\t\n  1716\tThe agent currently only stores facts about what it observes and does. But it doesn't store facts about _itself_ \u2014 its own reasoning patterns, what worked, what didn't, what strategies succeeded in what contexts. There's no learning across tasks or even within a long task.\n  1717\t\n  1718\tThe real leverage might be having the agent write assertions about its own epistemic state directly into the fact store \u2014 not just recording what happened, but capturing what it believes, where uncertainty lives, which strategies succeeded in which contexts. That transforms the fact store into a model of the agent's own cognition, enabling genuine metacognition. But even more powerful: the agent should be creating its own retrieval cues as datoms whenever it discovers connections between things, so those relationships become immediately queryable for future reasoning.\n  1719\t\n  1720\tHuman expertise works this way \u2014 experts don't just know more facts, they've built richer associative networks between them. When the agent writes its own edges into the EAV graph, the fact store becomes self-organizing and grows more useful with each discovery. The `associate` tool gets better over time because there are more paths to traverse, which is real learning through structural enrichment rather than parameter updates.\n  1721\t\n  1722\tThis mirrors Datomic's philosophy where the schema itself is data, so adding new relationships is just writing more datoms to the store. But I'm realizing the truly transformative piece might be something else entirely \u2014 letting the agent continuously build these associations in the background while reasoning, almost like System 1 running in parallel with System 2 to pre-fetch context. Though that feels more like an optimization than a fundamental shift. The core insight that matters most is probably just the self-authored edges themselves \u2014 giving the agent the ability to permanently reshape its own knowledge graph based on what it learns.\n  1723\t\n  1724\tActually, wait. What if the real breakthrough is even more basic: storing the agent's intentions and plans as first-class facts in the store? Right now it only records what happened, but if the agent's current goals and reasoning were structured data in there too, then `associate` could surface relevant plans automatically, confusion detection could flag when actions drift from stated intentions, and goal dilution becomes something you can actually query for.\n  1725\t\n  1726\tBut stepping back, which of these ideas is actually most powerful? The self-authored associations create a feedback loop where the system improves with use \u2014 that's fundamentally different from a one-time architectural fix. Though I'm wondering if there's something even more radical underneath all this...\n  1727\t\n  1728\tWhat if the fact store itself becomes the runtime?\n  1729\t\n  1730\tNo, that's getting too abstract. I think the answer is self-authored associations \u2014 where the agent writes its own relationship assertions into the knowledge graph, making it progressively more effective. That's the one that turns this from a static tool into a learning system with compounding returns.\n  1731\t\n  1732\tShow more\n  1733\t\n  1734\tDone\n  1735\t\n  1736\tThe agent should write its own edges into the EAV graph.\n  1737\t\n  1738\tThe Insight\n  1739\t-----------\n  1740\t\n  1741\tRight now the architecture has the agent as a **consumer** of the fact store. It reads datoms, queries structure, acts on the world. But the EAV graph's association topology \u2014 the edges that `associate` traverses \u2014 is determined entirely by the raw event history. Entity X is connected to entity Y only because they happened to co-occur in the same operation/observation pair.\n  1742\t\n  1743\tThis is impoverished. When a human expert debugs a system, they don't just observe that a config change and a TypeError co-occurred. They form an **assertion**: \"this kind of config change _causes_ this class of error.\" That learned association persists. The next time they see a similar config change, System 1 surfaces the error risk _before_ it manifests, because the association edge exists in their memory.\n  1744\t\n  1745\tCurrent agents can't do this. They observe correlations but never **reify them as persistent, queryable structure**. Every task starts with the same flat association topology. The agent never develops expertise.\n  1746\t\n  1747\tThe Mechanism\n  1748\t-------------\n  1749\t\n  1750\tAdd a tool \u2014 call it `assert` \u2014 that lets the agent write new datoms that are not observations of the external world but **assertions about relationships, patterns, causal links, and heuristics it has discovered**:\n  1751\t\n  1752\t    assert : [Datom] \u2192 FactStore \u2192 FactStore\n  1753\t    \n  1754\t    -- Examples of what the agent would write:\n  1755\t    \n  1756\t    (function:handle_request, :causally-linked-to, config:db-timeout,\n  1757\t     tx_57, true)\n  1758\t    \n  1759\t    (pattern:retry-loop, :resolves-with, strategy:backoff-and-check-logs,\n  1760\t     tx_83, true)\n  1761\t    \n  1762\t    (file:main.py, :architecturally-depends-on, file:schema.sql,\n  1763\t     tx_12, true)\n  1764\t    \n  1765\t    (error:type-error-42, :root-cause, change:removed-null-check,\n  1766\t     tx_91, true)\n  1767\t\n  1768\tThese datoms are **first-class citizens** in the store. They're immutable, temporally indexed, and \u2014 crucially \u2014 they're traversable by `associate`. The next time the agent is working near `config:db-timeout` and calls `associate`, it finds the causal link to `function:handle_request` that _the agent itself laid down_ during a previous task.\n  1769\t\n  1770\tWhy This Is the Highest-Leverage Addition\n  1771\t-----------------------------------------\n  1772\t\n  1773\tEverything else we've designed is a **constant-factor improvement**. Better context assembly, confusion detection, dual-process architecture \u2014 these make the agent better at each step, but each step starts from the same baseline.\n  1774\t\n  1775\tSelf-authored associations create a **positive feedback loop**:\n  1776\t\n  1777\t    More tasks completed \n  1778\t      \u2192 more associations asserted \n  1779\t        \u2192 richer EAV graph \n  1780\t          \u2192 better associate results \n  1781\t            \u2192 better context assembly \n  1782\t              \u2192 better task performance \n  1783\t                \u2192 more (and higher-quality) associations asserted\n  1784\t                  \u2192 ...\n  1785\t\n  1786\tThis is the mechanism by which a novice becomes an expert. Not more processing power, not better reasoning \u2014 **richer associative structure** that makes System 1 increasingly effective over time. The agent develops a **personalized ontology** shaped by its actual experience.\n  1787\t\n  1788\tThe Formal Property\n  1789\t-------------------\n  1790\t\n  1791\tIn graph-theoretic terms, the raw EAV graph from event logging alone has a specific connectivity structure determined by the co-occurrence patterns in `(Op, Obs)` pairs. Call this the **empirical graph** `G_e`.\n  1792\t\n  1793\tAgent-authored associations add edges that are not present in `G_e`. These edges represent **inferred structure** \u2014 causal links, architectural dependencies, strategic heuristics. Call the enriched graph `G_a = G_e + G_inferred`.\n  1794\t\n  1795\tThe `associate` tool traverses `G_a`, not `G_e`. So the agent's traversal space \u2014 the set of schema neighborhoods reachable from any seed \u2014 grows strictly with each assertion:\n  1796\t\n  1797\t    Reachable(G_e, seed, depth) \u2286 Reachable(G_a, seed, depth)\n  1798\t\n  1799\tAnd the inclusion is typically strict. A causal link like `(config:db-timeout :causally-linked-to function:handle_request)` connects two nodes that might be separated by many hops in `G_e` (or unreachable entirely if they never co-occurred in the same event). The agent-authored edge creates a **shortcut** in the association graph \u2014 precisely the \"chunk\" that expertise researchers identify as the unit of expert knowledge.\n  1800\t\n  1801\tWhat the Agent Actually Asserts\n  1802\t-------------------------------\n  1803\t\n  1804\tThis is the part that needs care. Not every observation should become a persistent assertion. The agent needs judgment about what's worth reifying. I'd categorize the high-value assertions as:\n  1805\t\n  1806\t**Causal links** \u2014 \"X caused Y.\" These are the most valuable because they enable predictive retrieval. If the agent learns that config changes cause type errors, `associate` surfaces error-handling context proactively when the agent is editing config.\n  1807\t\n  1808\t**Architectural dependencies** \u2014 \"X depends on Y.\" These prevent the goal dilution problem. If the agent has asserted that the API layer depends on the schema, then when it's modifying the schema, `associate` surfaces the API layer without the agent having to remember to check.\n  1809\t\n  1810\t**Strategic heuristics** \u2014 \"When facing situation X, strategy Y works.\" These are the highest-level assertions. They encode _what the agent has learned about how to be effective_. \"When tests fail after a refactor, check import paths first\" is a heuristic that, once asserted, biases future retrieval toward import-related facts when test failures occur post-refactor.\n  1811\t\n  1812\t**Retractions and corrections** \u2014 \"I previously believed X, but it was wrong.\" Because the store is append-only, the agent can retract earlier assertions. This is how the knowledge graph self-corrects. A retraction `(error:42, :root-cause, change:removed-null-check, tx_150, false)` doesn't delete the original assertion but marks it as no longer believed. The graph evolves.\n  1813\t\n  1814\tThe Compound Consequence\n  1815\t------------------------\n  1816\t\n\t< truncated lines 1817-1921 >\n  1922\t    {:e :decision/D001 :a :context :v \"discussed in session 2025-02-14\"}\n  1923\t\n  1924\tWhy this matters: six months later, someone (or the agent) encounters the event-sourced log and wonders \"why not just use a database?\" The answer is in the fact store, reachable by `associate` from any entity related to the log architecture. Without this, the rationale exists only in the raw JSONL \u2014 effectively lost.\n  1925\t\n  1926\t### 2\\. Invariants (Falsifiable Constraints)\n  1927\t\n  1928\tThese are the core of DDIS \u2014 the things that must remain true. Every invariant that crystallizes during conversation should be a datom, linked to what it constrains and how it's tested:\n  1929\t\n  1930\t    {:e :invariant/I001 :a :statement :v \"context window budget never exceeded\"}\n  1931\t    {:e :invariant/I001 :a :constrains :v :spec/context-assembly}\n  1932\t    {:e :invariant/I001 :a :falsified-by :v \"sum of layer allocations > w\"}\n  1933\t    {:e :invariant/I001 :a :emerged-from :v :decision/D003}\n  1934\t\n  1935\t### 3\\. Structural Dependencies\n  1936\t\n  1937\tThe relationships between spec components \u2014 what depends on what, what constrains what, what must change together:\n  1938\t\n  1939\t    {:e :spec/context-assembly :a :depends-on :v :spec/fact-store}\n  1940\t    {:e :spec/fact-store :a :must-coevolve-with :v :spec/query-interface}\n  1941\t    {:e :spec/retrieval-policy :a :parameterized-by :v :spec/confusion-detector}\n  1942\t\n  1943\tThese are the edges that make `associate` powerful. Without them, the fact store is a collection of isolated facts. With them, it's a navigable knowledge graph.\n  1944\t\n  1945\t### 4\\. Learned Heuristics\n  1946\t\n  1947\tThings the agent (or you) discovered during the process that aren't part of the spec but inform future work:\n  1948\t\n  1949\t    {:e :heuristic/H001 :a :observation \n  1950\t     :v \"invariants are easier to write after the approach is decided\"}\n  1951\t    {:e :heuristic/H001 :a :applies-to :v :process/ddis-discovery}\n  1952\t    \n  1953\t    {:e :heuristic/H002 :a :observation \n  1954\t     :v \"LLM loses track of goal after ~40 tool calls without compaction\"}\n  1955\t    {:e :heuristic/H002 :a :applies-to :v :spec/agent-loop}\n  1956\t\n  1957\tHow to Decide (The Extraction Heuristic)\n  1958\t----------------------------------------\n  1959\t\n  1960\tGiven these four categories, here's the practical test for whether something in the JSONL log deserves to become a datom:\n  1961\t\n  1962\t**The reversibility test.** If this fact were lost, would someone need to re-derive it from first principles? If yes, it's a datom. If no \u2014 if it's obvious from context or trivially re-derivable \u2014 it's conversational noise.\n  1963\t\n  1964\tApplied:\n  1965\t\n  1966\t*   \"What about using OAuth?\" \u2192 noise (exploratory question)\n  1967\t*   \"We're going with OAuth2 PKCE because API keys can't rotate without downtime\" \u2192 **decision + rationale** (expensive to re-derive, encodes reasoning)\n  1968\t*   \"Ok let me think about that\" \u2192 noise (conversational mechanics)\n  1969\t*   \"The context window must never be exceeded\" \u2192 **invariant** (core constraint)\n  1970\t*   \"I noticed the LLM loses coherence after long tool-call sequences\" \u2192 **heuristic** (learned through experience, not obvious a priori)\n  1971\t\n  1972\tThe Extraction Process Itself\n  1973\t-----------------------------\n  1974\t\n  1975\tHere's where the architecture eats its own tail. The agent that's helping you develop DDIS specs should be the one extracting datoms from the conversation. This is a natural application of the `assert` mechanism we defined:\n  1976\t\n  1977\t    At the end of each discovery session (or at natural breakpoints):\n  1978\t    \n  1979\t    Agent reviews the JSONL log for the session\n  1980\t    Agent identifies decisions, invariants, dependencies, heuristics\n  1981\t    Agent proposes datoms (shows them to you for approval)\n  1982\t    You approve/edit/reject\n  1983\t    Approved datoms are appended to .ddis/facts.ednl\n  1984\t    Git commit with descriptive message\n  1985\t\n  1986\tThe agent is performing exactly the operation we described: **writing its own edges into the knowledge graph based on what it learned during the session**. And because you're in the approval loop, the graph quality is human-supervised.\n  1987\t\n  1988\tOver time, as the fact store grows and `associate` becomes available, the agent starts each new session with richer context. It can query decisions made in earlier sessions, check whether a new proposal contradicts an existing invariant, and surface relevant heuristics from past experience. The flywheel turns.\n  1989\t\n  1990\tThe Git-Native Query Layer\n  1991\t--------------------------\n  1992\t\n  1993\tThe remaining practical question: how do you query this file? You don't want to run a Datomic instance just to read a text file in your repo.\n  1994\t\n  1995\tFor a first implementation, I think you need surprisingly little:\n  1996\t\n  1997\tbash\n  1998\t\n  1999\t    # All decisions about auth:\n  2000\t    grep ':spec/auth' .ddis/facts.ednl\n  2001\t    \n  2002\t    # All invariants:\n  2003\t    grep ':invariant/' .ddis/facts.ednl\n  2004\t    \n  2005\t    # Everything connected to the fact store spec:\n  2006\t    grep ':spec/fact-store' .ddis/facts.ednl\n  2007\t\n  2008\tThis is crude but functional. The flat-file format means `grep` is your baseline Datalog. For richer queries, a small script that parses the EDN lines into an in-memory structure and supports basic pattern matching would suffice:\n  2009\t\n  2010\tpython\n  2011\t\n  2012\t    # Load facts\n  2013\t    facts = [parse_edn(line) for line in open('.ddis/facts.ednl')]\n  2014\t    \n  2015\t    # \"What depends on the fact store?\"\n  2016\t    [f for f in facts if f['a'] == ':depends-on' and f['v'] == ':spec/fact-store']\n  2017\t    \n  2018\t    # \"What was decided and why?\" (two-hop join)\n  2019\t    decisions = {f['e'] for f in facts if f['a'] == ':chose'}\n  2020\t    rationales = [f for f in facts if f['e'] in decisions and f['a'] == ':because']\n  2021\t\n  2022\tThe point is: **you don't need Datomic on day one**. You need a file in git, a convention for what goes in it, and a 50-line script that the agent can call as a tool. If the approach validates, you migrate to a proper Datalog engine. The file format is already datom-shaped, so the migration is mechanical.\n  2023\t\n  2024\tWhat I'd Actually Build First\n  2025\t-----------------------------\n  2026\t\n  2027\tIf this were my next concrete step:\n  2028\t\n  2029\t1. At the end of your next discovery session, have the agent extract datoms from the JSONL log using the four-category heuristic. Review and commit.\n  2031\t3. Write the `associate` tool as a slightly smarter version \u2014 given a seed term, find all datoms whose entity, attribute, or value matches semantically, then follow `:depends-on`, `:constrains`, `:causally-linked-to` edges one hop out.\n  2033\t5.  Start the next session with the agent calling `associate` on the current topic before diving in.\n  2034\t\n  2035\tThat's the minimal loop. Does this feel actionable, or is there a constraint I'm missing \u2014 particularly around how the JSONL logs are currently structured?\n  2036\t\n  2037\t---\n  2038\t\n  2039\t\n[2039 lines total]",

Right now it only records what happened, but if the agent's current goals and reasoning were structured data in there too, then `associate` could surface relevant plans automatically, confusion detection could flag when actions drift from stated intentions, and goal dilution becomes something you can actually query for.\\n\\nBut stepping back, which of these ideas is actually most powerful? The self-authored associations create a feedback loop where the system improves with use \\u2014 that's fundamentally different from a one-time architectural fix. Though I'm wondering if there's something even more radical underneath all this...\\n\\nWhat if the fact store itself becomes the runtime?\\n\\nNo, that's getting too abstract. I think the answer is self-authored associations \\u2014 where the agent writes its own relationship assertions into the knowledge graph, making it progressively more effective. That's the one that turns this from a static tool into a learning system with compounding returns.\\n\\nShow more\\n\\nDone\\n\\nThe agent should write its own edges into the EAV graph.\\n\\nThe Insight\\n-----------\\n\\nRight now the architecture has the agent as a **consumer** of the fact store. It reads datoms, queries structure, acts on the world. But the EAV graph's association topology \\u2014 the edges that `associate` traverses \\u2014 is determined entirely by the raw event history. Entity X is connected to entity Y only because they happened to co-occur in the same operation/observation pair.\\n\\nThis is impoverished. When a human expert debugs a system, they don't just observe that a config change and a TypeError co-occurred. They form an **assertion**: \\\"this kind of config change _causes_ this class of error.\\\" That learned association persists. The next time they see a similar config change, System 1 surfaces the error risk _before_ it manifests, because the association edge exists in their memory.\\n\\nCurrent agents can't do this. They observe correlations but never **reify them as persistent, queryable structure**. Every task starts with the same flat association topology. The agent never develops expertise.\\n\\nThe Mechanism\\n-------------\\n\\nAdd a tool \\u2014 call it `assert` \\u2014 that lets the agent write new datoms that are not observations of the external world but **assertions about relationships, patterns, causal links, and heuristics it has discovered**:\\n\\n    assert : [Datom] \\u2192 FactStore \\u2192 FactStore\\n    \\n    -- Examples of what the agent would write:\\n    \\n    (function:handle_request, :causally-linked-to, config:db-timeout,\\n     tx_57, true)\\n    \\n    (pattern:retry-loop, :resolves-with, strategy:backoff-and-check-logs,\\n     tx_83, true)\\n    \\n    (file:main.py, :architecturally-depends-on, file:schema.sql,\\n     tx_12, true)\\n    \\n    (error:type-error-42, :root-cause, change:removed-null-check,\\n     tx_91, true)\\n\\nThese datoms are **first-class citizens** in the store. They're immutable, temporally indexed, and \\u2014 crucially \\u2014 they're traversable by `associate`. The next time the agent is working near `config:db-timeout` and calls `associate`, it finds the causal link to `function:handle_request` that _the agent itself laid down_ during a previous task.\\n\\nWhy This Is the Highest-Leverage Addition\\n-----------------------------------------\\n\\nEverything else we've designed is a **constant-factor improvement**. Better context assembly, confusion detection, dual-process architecture \\u2014 these make the agent better at each step, but each step starts from the same baseline.\\n\\nSelf-authored associations create a **positive feedback loop**:\\n\\n    More tasks completed \\n      \\u2192 more associations asserted \\n        \\u2192 richer EAV graph \\n          \\u2192 better associate results \\n            \\u2192 better context assembly \\n              \\u2192 better task performance \\n                \\u2192 more (and higher-quality) associations asserted\\n                  \\u2192 ...\\n\\nThis is the mechanism by which a novice becomes an expert. Not more processing power, not better reasoning \\u2014 **richer associative structure** that makes System 1 increasingly effective over time. The agent develops a **personalized ontology** shaped by its actual experience.\\n\\nThe Formal Property\\n-------------------\\n\\nIn graph-theoretic terms, the raw EAV graph from event logging alone has a specific connectivity structure determined by the co-occurrence patterns in `(Op, Obs)` pairs. Call this the **empirical graph** `G_e`.\\n\\nAgent-authored associations add edges that are not present in `G_e`. These edges represent **inferred structure** \\u2014 causal links, architectural dependencies, strategic heuristics. Call the enriched graph `G_a = G_e + G_inferred`.\\n\\nThe `associate` tool traverses `G_a`, not `G_e`. So the agent's traversal space \\u2014 the set of schema neighborhoods reachable from any seed \\u2014 grows strictly with each assertion:\\n\\n    Reachable(G_e, seed, depth) \\u2286 Reachable(G_a, seed, depth)\\n\\nAnd the inclusion is typically strict. A causal link like `(config:db-timeout :causally-linked-to function:handle_request)` connects two nodes that might be separated by many hops in `G_e` (or unreachable entirely if they never co-occurred in the same event). The agent-authored edge creates a **shortcut** in the association graph \\u2014 precisely the \\\"chunk\\\" that expertise researchers identify as the unit of expert knowledge.\\n\\nWhat the Agent Actually Asserts\\n-------------------------------\\n\\nThis is the part that needs care. Not every observation should become a persistent assertion. The agent needs judgment about what's worth reifying. I'd categorize the high-value assertions as:\\n\\n**Causal links** \\u2014 \\\"X caused Y.\\\" These are the most valuable because they enable predictive retrieval. If the agent learns that config changes cause type errors, `associate` surfaces error-handling context proactively when the agent is editing config.\\n\\n**Architectural dependencies** \\u2014 \\\"X depends on Y.\\\" These prevent the goal dilution problem. If the agent has asserted that the API layer depends on the schema, then when it's modifying the schema, `associate` surfaces the API layer without the agent having to remember to check.\\n\\n**Strategic heuristics** \\u2014 \\\"When facing situation X, strategy Y works.\\\" These are the highest-level assertions. They encode _what the agent has learned about how to be effective_. \\\"When tests fail after a refactor, check import paths first\\\" is a heuristic that, once asserted, biases future retrieval toward import-related facts when test failures occur post-refactor.\\n\\n**Retractions and corrections** \\u2014 \\\"I previously believed X, but it was wrong.\\\" Because the store is append-only, the agent can retract earlier assertions. This is how the knowledge graph self-corrects. A retraction `(error:42, :root-cause, change:removed-null-check, tx_150, false)` doesn't delete the original assertion but marks it as no longer believed. The graph evolves.\\n\\nThe Compound Consequence\\n------------------------\\n\\n< truncated lines 1817-1921 >\\n    {:e :decision/D001 :a :context :v \\\"discussed in session 2025-02-14\\\"}\\n\\nWhy this matters: six months later, someone (or the agent) encounters the event-sourced log and wonders \\\"why not just use a database?\\\" The answer is in the fact store, reachable by `associate` from any entity related to the log architecture. Without this, the rationale exists only in the raw JSONL \\u2014 effectively lost.\\n\\n### 2\\\\. Invariants (Falsifiable Constraints)\\n\\nThese are the core of DDIS \\u2014 the things that must remain true. Every invariant that crystallizes during conversation should be a datom, linked to what it constrains and how it's tested:\\n\\n    {:e :invariant/I001 :a :statement :v \\\"context window budget never exceeded\\\"}\\n    {:e :invariant/I001 :a :constrains :v :spec/context-assembly}\\n    {:e :invariant/I001 :a :falsified-by :v \\\"sum of layer allocations > w\\\"}\\n    {:e :invariant/I001 :a :emerged-from :v :decision/D003}\\n\\n### 3\\\\. Structural Dependencies\\n\\nThe relationships between spec components \\u2014 what depends on what, what constrains what, what must change together:\\n\\n    {:e :spec/context-assembly :a :depends-on :v :spec/fact-store}\\n    {:e :spec/fact-store :a :must-coevolve-with :v :spec/query-interface}\\n    {:e :spec/retrieval-policy :a :parameterized-by :v :spec/confusion-detector}\\n\\nThese are the edges that make `associate` powerful. Without them, the fact store is a collection of isolated facts. With them, it's a navigable knowledge graph.\\n\\n### 4\\\\. Learned Heuristics\\n\\nThings the agent (or you) discovered during the process that aren't part of the spec but inform future work:\\n\\n    {:e :heuristic/H001 :a :observation \\n     :v \\\"invariants are easier to write after the approach is decided\\\"}\\n    {:e :heuristic/H001 :a :applies-to :v :process/ddis-discovery}\\n    \\n    {:e :heuristic/H002 :a :observation \\n     :v \\\"LLM loses track of goal after ~40 tool calls without compaction\\\"}\\n    {:e :heuristic/H002 :a :applies-to :v :spec/agent-loop}\\n\\nHow to Decide (The Extraction Heuristic)\\n----------------------------------------\\n\\nGiven these four categories, here's the practical test for whether something in the JSONL log deserves to become a datom:\\n\\n**The reversibility test.** If this fact were lost, would someone need to re-derive it from first principles? If yes, it's a datom. If no \\u2014 if it's obvious from context or trivially re-derivable \\u2014 it's conversational noise.\\n\\nApplied:\\n\\n*   \\\"What about using OAuth?\\\" \\u2192 noise (exploratory question)\\n*   \\\"We're going with OAuth2 PKCE because API keys can't rotate without downtime\\\" \\u2192 **decision + rationale** (expensive to re-derive, encodes reasoning)\\n*   \\\"Ok let me think about that\\\" \\u2192 noise (conversational mechanics)\\n*   \\\"The context window must never be exceeded\\\" \\u2192 **invariant** (core constraint)\\n*   \\\"I noticed the LLM loses coherence after long tool-call sequences\\\" \\u2192 **heuristic** (learned through experience, not obvious a priori)\\n\\nThe Extraction Process Itself\\n-----------------------------\\n\\nHere's where the architecture eats its own tail. The agent that's helping you develop DDIS specs should be the one extracting datoms from the conversation. This is a natural application of the `assert` mechanism we defined:\\n\\n    At the end of each discovery session (or at natural breakpoints):\\n    \\n    Agent reviews the JSONL log for the session\\n    Agent identifies decisions, invariants, dependencies, heuristics\\n    Agent proposes datoms (shows them to you for approval)\\n    You approve/edit/reject\\n    Approved datoms are appended to .ddis/facts.ednl\\n    Git commit with descriptive message\\n\\nThe agent is performing exactly the operation we described: **writing its own edges into the knowledge graph based on what it learned during the session**. And because you're in the approval loop, the graph quality is human-supervised.\\n\\nOver time, as the fact store grows and `associate` becomes available, the agent starts each new session with richer context. It can query decisions made in earlier sessions, check whether a new proposal contradicts an existing invariant, and surface relevant heuristics from past experience. The flywheel turns.\\n\\nThe Git-Native Query Layer\\n--------------------------\\n\\nThe remaining practical question: how do you query this file? You don't want to run a Datomic instance just to read a text file in your repo.\\n\\nFor a first implementation, I think you need surprisingly little:\\n\\nbash\\n\\n    # All decisions about auth:\\n    grep ':spec/auth' .ddis/facts.ednl\\n    \\n    # All invariants:\\n    grep ':invariant/' .ddis/facts.ednl\\n    \\n    # Everything connected to the fact store spec:\\n    grep ':spec/fact-store' .ddis/facts.ednl\\n\\nThis is crude but functional. The flat-file format means `grep` is your baseline Datalog. For richer queries, a small script that parses the EDN lines into an in-memory structure and supports basic pattern matching would suffice:\\n\\npython\\n\\n    # Load facts\\n    facts = [parse_edn(line) for line in open('.ddis/facts.ednl')]\\n    \\n    # \\\"What depends on the fact store?\\\"\\n    [f for f in facts if f['a'] == ':depends-on' and f['v'] == ':spec/fact-store']\\n    \\n    # \\\"What was decided and why?\\\" (two-hop join)\\n    decisions = {f['e'] for f in facts if f['a'] == ':chose'}\\n    rationales = [f for f in facts if f['e'] in decisions and f['a'] == ':because']\\n\\nThe point is: **you don't need Datomic on day one**. You need a file in git, a convention for what goes in it, and a 50-line script that the agent can call as a tool. If the approach validates, you migrate to a proper Datalog engine. The file format is already datom-shaped, so the migration is mechanical.\\n\\nWhat I'd Actually Build First\\n-----------------------------\\n\\nIf this were my next concrete step:\\n\\n1.  Add a `facts.ednl` (or `facts.jsonl` if you prefer, the format is secondary) to the DDIS repo.\\n2.  At the end of your next discovery session, have the agent extract datoms from the JSONL log using the four-category heuristic. Review and commit.\\n3.  Write a minimal query tool (the 50-line script) and expose it to the agent.\\n4.  Write the `associate` tool as a slightly smarter version \\u2014 given a seed term, find all datoms whose entity, attribute, or value matches semantically, then follow `:depends-on`, `:constrains`, `:causally-linked-to` edges one hop out.\\n5.  Start the next session with the agent calling `associate` on the current topic before diving in.\\n\\nThat's the minimal loop. Does this feel actionable, or is there a constraint I'm missing \\u2014 particularly around how the JSONL logs are currently structured?\\n\\n---\\n\\n\", \"filename\": \"/mnt/user-data/uploads/Formal_analysis_of_agentic_system_architecture.md\"}"

Commits are immutable. Every commit has a timestamp. The reflog gives you full history. The working tree is a materialized view of the current state. This is structurally closer to Datomic than most people recognize.\\n\\nThe difference is that git stores **files**, not **datoms**. So the question becomes: what file format in git gives you EAV semantics with Datalog-like queryability, while remaining human-readable, diffable, and mergeable?\\n\\nI think the answer is: **one datom per line, in a simple structured text format, stored in git**.\\n\\n    # file: .ddis/facts.ednl  (or .jsonl, but EDN is more natural for Datalog)\\n    \\n    {:e :spec/auth-module :a :decided-at :v \\\"2025-02-14\\\" :tx 1}\\n    {:e :spec/auth-module :a :approach :v \\\"OAuth2 PKCE\\\" :tx 1}\\n    {:e :spec/auth-module :a :rejected-alternative :v \\\"API key\\\" :tx 1}\\n    {:e :spec/auth-module :a :rationale :v \\\"API keys lack rotation without downtime\\\" :tx 1}\\n    {:e :decision/use-pkce :a :depends-on :v :constraint/zero-downtime-rotation :tx 1}\\n    {:e :spec/auth-module :a :invariant :v \\\"token refresh must complete in <200ms\\\" :tx 2}\\n    {:e :invariant/token-refresh-latency :a :falsifiable-by :v \\\"load test at 95th percentile\\\" :tx 2}\\n\\nEach line is a datom. The file is append-only (you only add lines, never edit or remove \\u2014 retraction is a new line with `:retracted true`). Git tracks the history. Diffs are meaningful \\u2014 a commit that adds five datoms shows exactly five new lines. Merges work because append-only files have trivial merge semantics (concatenate).\\n\\nThe git commit becomes the transaction. The full machinery of git \\u2014 branches, tags, blame, log \\u2014 becomes your temporal query interface.\\n\\nWhat Goes In\\n------------\\n\\nThis is the question you're actually stuck on, and it's the right question. This is a remarkably convergent piece of work \u2014 you've been building toward the same architecture from two independent directions, and the places where the two lines of reasoning *independently agree* are the strongest signal that we're on the right track. But there are also places where this document reveals gaps in our plan, and places where our plan addresses gaps the document identifies but doesn't resolve. Let me lay it out.\n\n---\n\n## Where the Two Converge (Independent Validation)\n\nThe document arrives at **datom-native fact stores replacing JSONL** from a completely different starting point than ours. We got there by asking \"what's the right CRDT for multi-agent coordination?\" The other conversation got there by asking \"what's the optimal epistemic substrate for a single agent?\" Both arrive at the same answer: append-only EAV with Datalog queries and schema-on-read. When two independent derivations converge on the same structure, it's usually because the structure is capturing something real.\n\nThe document's tripartite decomposition **(E, R, A)** \u2014 event log, runtime, agent \u2014 maps exactly onto our architecture:\n\n- **E** = the datom store (our G-Set CRDT over D = E \u00d7 A \u00d7 V \u00d7 T \u00d7 Op)\n- **R** = the runtime environment (filesystem, shell, tools)  \n- **A** = the LLM agent with tool-calling (the policy function \u03c0)\n\nAnd the document's formal specification `AgentSystem = (E, Op, Exo, Obs, S, \u03c0, \u03b4, \u03c6)` is *precisely* the single-agent specialization of our multi-agent system. Our protocol (part C) is what you get when you generalize this to N agents sharing the same E, each with their own local frontier, and needing to coordinate.\n\nThe `assert` mechanism the document proposes \u2014 agents writing their own edges into the knowledge graph \u2014 is exactly what our transaction protocol needs to support.
